                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2011 43:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The Brassica rapa Genome Sequencing Project Consortium reports the draft genome of the B. rapa accession Chiifu-401-42, an inbred Chinese cabbage line. The B. rapa genome should provide a useful reference genome for the Brassica species, which include many important oil and vegetable crops. We report the annotation and analysis of the draft genome sequence of Brassica rapa accession Chiifu-401-42, a Chinese cabbage. We modeled 41,174 protein coding genes in the B. rapa genome, which has undergone genome triplication. We used Arabidopsis thaliana as an outgroup for investigating the consequences of genome triplication, such as structural and functional evolution. The extent of gene loss (fractionation) among triplicated genome segments varies, with one of the three copies consistently retaining a disproportionately large fraction of the genes expected to have been present in its ancestor. Variation in the number of members of gene families present in the genome may contribute to the remarkable morphological plasticity of Brassica species. The B. rapa genome sequence provides an important resource for studying the evolution of polyploid genomes and underpins the genetic improvement of Brassica oil and vegetable crops."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>We report the annotation and analysis of the draft genome sequence of <i>Brassica rapa</i> accession Chiifu-401-42, a Chinese cabbage. We modeled 41,174 protein coding genes in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome, which has undergone genome triplication. We used <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> as an outgroup for investigating the consequences of genome triplication, such as structural and functional evolution. The extent of gene loss (fractionation) among triplicated genome segments varies, with one of the three copies consistently retaining a disproportionately large fraction of the genes expected to have been present in its ancestor. Variation in the number of members of gene families present in the genome may contribute to the remarkable morphological plasticity of <i>Brassica</i> species. The <i>B. rapa</i> genome sequence provides an important resource for studying the evolution of polyploid genomes and underpins the genetic improvement of <i>Brassica</i> oil and vegetable crops.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Model species have provided valuable insights into angiosperm (flowering plant) genome structure, function and evolution. For example, <i>A. thaliana</i> has experienced two genome duplications since its divergence from <i>Carica</i>, with rapid DNA sequence divergence, extensive gene loss and fractionation of ancestral gene order eroding the resemblance of <i>A. thaliana</i> to ancestral Brassicales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2045">1</a></sup>. Compared with an ancestor at just a few million years ago, <i>A. thaliana</i> has undergone a <span class="stix">∼</span>30% reduction in genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Johnston, J.S. et al. Evolution of genome size in Brassicaceae. Ann. Bot. 95, 229–235 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2053">2</a></sup> and 9–10 chromosomal rearrangements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Koch, M.A. &amp; Kiefer, M. Genome evolution among cruciferous plants: a lecture from the comparison of the genetic maps of three diploid species—Capsella rubella, Arabidopsis lyrata subsp Petraea, and A. thaliana. Am. J. Bot. 92, 761–767 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2057">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yogeeswaran, K. et al. Comparative genome analyses of Arabidopsis spp.: inferring chromosomal rearrangement events in the evolutionary history of A. thaliana. Genome Res. 15, 505–515 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2060">4</a></sup> that differentiate it from its sister species <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>. Whole-genome duplication has been observed in all plant genomes sequenced to date. <i>A. thaliana</i> has undergone three paleo-polyploidy events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Bowers, J.E., Chapman, B.A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433–438 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2070">5</a></sup>: a paleohexaploidy (γ) event shared with most dicots (asterids and rosids) and two paleotetraploidy events (β then α) shared with other members of the order Brassicales. <i>B. rapa</i> shares this complex history but with the addition of a whole-genome triplication (WGT) thought to have occurred between 13 and 17 million years ago (MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yang, Y.W., Lai, K.N., Tai, P.Y. &amp; Li, W.H. Rates of nucleotide substitution in angiosperm mitochondrial DNA sequences and dates of divergence between Brassica and other angiosperm lineages. J. Mol. Evol. 48, 597–604 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2078">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Town, C.D. et al. Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell 18, 1348–1359 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2081">7</a></sup>, making 'mesohexaploidy' a characteristic of the Brassiceae tribe of the Brassicaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Lysak, M.A., Koch, M.A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516–525 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2085">8</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Brassica</i> crops are used for human nutrition and provide opportunities for the study of genome evolution. These crops include important vegetables (<i>B. rapa</i> (Chinese cabbage, pak choi and turnip) and <i>Brassica oleracea</i> (broccoli, cabbage and cauliflower)) as well as oilseed crops (<i>Brassica napus</i>, <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>Brassica juncea</i> and <i>Brassica carinata</i>), which provide collectively 12% of the world's edible vegetable oil production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Labana, K.S. &amp; Gupta, M.L. Importance and origin. in Breeding Oilseed Brassicas (eds. Labana, K.S., Banga, S.S. &amp; Banga, S.K.) 1–20 (Springer-Verlag, Berlin, Germany, 1993)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2113">9</a></sup>. The six widely cultivated <i>Brassica</i> species are also a classical example of the importance of polyploidy in botanical evolution, described by 'U's triangle'<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Nagaharu, U. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of B. napus and peculiar mode of fertilization. Jap. J. Bot. 7, 389–452 (1935)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2120">10</a></sup>, with the three diploid species <i>B. rapa</i> (A genome), <i>Brassica nigra</i> (B genome) and <i>B. oleracea</i> (C genome) having formed the amphidiploid species <i>B. juncea</i> (A and B genomes), <i>B. napus</i> (A and C genomes) and <i>B. carinata</i> (B and C genomes) by hybridization. Comparative physical mapping studies have confirmed genome triplication in a common ancestor of <i>B. oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="O'Neill, C.M. &amp; Bancroft, I. Comparative physical mapping of segments of the genome of Brassica oleracea var. alboglabra that are homoeologous to sequenced regions of chromosomes 4 and 5 of Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant J. 23, 233–243 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2145">11</a></sup> and <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Park, J.Y. et al. Physical mapping and microsynteny of Brassica rapa ssp. pekinensis genome corresponding to a 222 kbp gene-rich region of Arabidopsis chromosome 4 and partially duplicated on chromosome 5. Mol. Genet. Genomics 274, 579–588 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2152">12</a></sup> since its divergence from the <i>A. thaliana</i> lineage at least 13–17 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yang, Y.W., Lai, K.N., Tai, P.Y. &amp; Li, W.H. Rates of nucleotide substitution in angiosperm mitochondrial DNA sequences and dates of divergence between Brassica and other angiosperm lineages. J. Mol. Evol. 48, 597–604 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2159">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Town, C.D. et al. Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell 18, 1348–1359 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2162">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Beilstein, M.A., Nagalingum, N.S., Clements, M.D., Manchester, S.R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724–18728 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2165">13</a></sup>.</p><p>Using 72× coverage of paired short read sequences generated by Illumina GA II technology and stringent assembly parameters, we assembled the genome of the <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>pekinensis</i> line Chiifu-401-42 and analyzed the assembly (Online Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). The final assembly statistics are summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Tab1">Table 1</a>. The assembled sequence of 283.8 Mb was estimated to cover &gt;98% of the gene space (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 1</a>) and is greater than the previous estimated size of the euchromatic space, 220 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Mun, J.H. et al. Genome-wide comparative analysis of the Brassica rapa gene space reveals genome shrinkage and differential loss of duplicated genes after whole genome triplication. Genome Biol. 10, R111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2188">14</a></sup>. The assembly showed excellent agreement with the previously reported chromosome A03 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mun, J.H. et al. Sequence and structure of Brassica rapa chromosome A3. Genome Biol. 11, R94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2191">15</a>) and with 647 bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Mun, J.H. et al. Genome-wide comparative analysis of the Brassica rapa gene space reveals genome shrinkage and differential loss of duplicated genes after whole genome triplication. Genome Biol. 10, R111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2195">14</a></sup> (Online Methods) sequenced by Sanger technology. Integration with 199,452 BAC-end sequences produced 159 super scaffolds representing 90% of the assembled sequences, with an N50 scaffold (N50 scaffold is a weighted median statistic indicating that 50% of the entire assembly is contained in scaffolds equal to or larger than this value) size of 1.97 Mb. Genetic mapping of 1,427 markers in <i>B. rapa</i> allowed us to produce ten pseudo chromosomes that included 90% of the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the final assembly statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.919/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We found the difference in the physical sizes of the <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> genomes to be largely because of transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Although widely dispersed throughout the genome, as shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Fig1">Figure 1</a>, the transposon-related sequences were most abundant in the vicinity of the centromeres. We estimated that transposon-related sequences occupy 39.5% of the genome, with the proportions of retrotransposons (with long terminal repeats), DNA transposons and long interspersed elements being 27.1%, 3.2% and 2.8%, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Tables 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Chromosomal distribution of the main B. rapa genome features."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Chromosomal distribution of the main <i>B. rapa</i> genome features.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="929"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Area charts quantify retrotransposons, genes (exons and introns) and DNA transposons. The <i>x</i> axis denotes the physical position along the <i>B. rapa</i> chromosomes in units of million (M) bases.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We modeled and analyzed protein coding genes (described in the Online Methods and the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). We identified 41,174 protein coding genes, distributed as shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Fig1">Figure 1</a>. The gene models have an average transcript length of 2,015 bp, a coding length of 1,172 bp and a mean of 5.03 exons per gene, both similar to that observed in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2549">16</a></sup>. A total of 95.8% of gene models have a match in at least one of the public protein databases and 99.3% are represented among the public EST collections or <i>de novo</i> Illumina mRNA-Seq data. Among the total 16,917 <i>B. rapa</i> gene families, only 1,003 (5.9%) appear to be lineage specific, with 15,725 (93.0%) shared with <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2562">16</a></sup> and 9,909 (58.6%) also shared by <i>Carica papaya</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991–996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2568">17</a></sup> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2574">18</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="537"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families between and among four sequenced dicotyledonous species (<i>B. rapa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We analyzed the organization and evolution of the genome (as described in the Online Methods and the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). <i>B. rapa</i>'s close relationship to <i>A. thaliana</i> allows <i>Arabidopsis</i> to be used as an outgroup for investigating the adaptation of the <i>Brassica</i> lineage to the triplicated state. In total, 108.6 Mb (90.01%) of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome and 259.6 Mb (91.13%) of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome assembly were contained within collinear blocks. We confirmed the almost complete triplication of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome relative to <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>) and (by inference) to the postulated Brassicaceae ancestral genome (<i>n</i> = 8). The gene paralogues anchored in the triplicated segments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) and their orthologs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 6</a>) dated the mesohexaploidy event to between 5 and 9 MYA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), which is more recent than has been reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Beilstein, M.A., Nagalingum, N.S., Clements, M.D., Manchester, S.R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724–18728 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2659">13</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Segmental collinearity of the genomes of B. rapa and A. thaliana."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Segmental collinearity of the genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="353"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Conserved collinear blocks of gene models are shown between the ten chromosomes of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome (horizontal axis) and the five chromosomes of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome (vertical axis). These blocks are labeled A to X and are color coded by inferred ancestral chromosome following established convention.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>Brassica</i> mesohexaploidy offers an opportunity to study gene retention in triplicated genomes. Assuming an initial count of protein coding genes similar to that of <i>A. thaliana</i> (around 30,000), the newly formed hexaploid would have about 90,000 genes, of which we can now identify only 41,174. This is typical of the substantial gene loss that occurs following polyploid formation in eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Sankoff, D., Zheng, C. &amp; Zhu, Q. The collapse of gene complement following whole genome duplication. BMC Genomics 11, 313 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2705">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Messing, J. et al. Sequence composition and genome organization of maize. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 14349–14354 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2708">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Schnable, J.C., Springer, N.M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2711">21</a></sup>. We identified each of the orthologous blocks in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome corresponding to ancestral blocks using collinearity between orthologs on the genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> and found significant disparity in gene loss across the triplicated blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Of the 21 regions of conserved synteny, 20 showed significant deviations from equivalent gene frequencies (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). To illustrate this variation, we concatenated the least fractionated blocks (LF), the medium fractionated blocks (MF1) and the most fractionated blocks (MF2) and calculated the proportions of genes retained in each of these sub-genomes relative to <i>A. thaliana</i>. The LF sub-genome retains 70% of the genes found in <i>A. thaliana</i>, whereas the MF1 and MF2 sub-genomes retain substantially lower proportions of retained genes (46% and 36%, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Based on the analysis of synonymous base substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values), the pairwise divergences between the three sub-genomes are indistinguishable from each other (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Our observation of differentially fractionated sub-genomes is consistent with the hypothesis that the sub-genomes MF1 and MF2 underwent substantial fractionation in a tetraploid nucleus before fractionation commenced in the LF genome in a more recently formed hexaploid. However, biased fractionation following tetraploidy (albeit less extreme than we observed) has been reported in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Thomas, B.C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2753">22</a></sup> and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Woodhouse, M.R. et al. Following tetraploidy in maize, a short deletion mechanism removed genes preferentially from one of the two homologs. PLoS Biol. 8, e1000409 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2757">23</a></sup>, where it was hypothesized to be the result of differential epigenetic marking of the parent genomes (resulting in differential gene silencing and consequential fraction), representing an alternative hypothesis.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="The density of orthologous genes in three subgenomes (LF, MF1 and MF2) of B. rapa compared to A. thaliana."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: The density of orthologous genes in three subgenomes (LF, MF1 and MF2) of <i>B. rapa</i> compared to <i>A. thaliana.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="377"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The <i>x</i> axis denotes the physical position of each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene locus. The <i>y</i> axis denotes the percentage of retained orthologous genes in <i>B. rapa</i> subgenomes around each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene, where 500 genes flanking each side of a certain gene locus were analyzed, giving a total window size of 1,001 genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The retention of extensive collinear genome blocks provides a potential opportunity for ectopic DNA recombination. By finding and comparing homologous gene quartets, including two α or β duplicates in <i>Brassica</i> and their respective orthologs in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, we noted that, respectively, 25% and 30% of <i>Brassica</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> duplicates are more similar to their intragenomic paralog than to their intergenomic ortholog, suggesting appreciable gene conversion since the divergence of these lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). The sizes of the affected regions vary from 10 bp to &gt;2 kb, with a majority of these apparent conversion events occurring in parallel in both species. Genes proximal to telomeres tend to have lower nucleotide substitution rates than distal genes (<i>P</i> = 0.0004), which is likely to be a result of higher conversion rates in the former and is consistent with prior findings in grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wang, X., Tang, H., Bowers, J.E. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Comparative inference of illegitimate recombination between rice and sorghum duplicated genes produced by polyploidization. Genome Res. 19, 1026–1032 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2825">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, X.Y., Tang, H.B. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Seventy million years of concerted evolution of a homoeologous chromosome pair, in parallel, in major poaceae lineages. Plant Cell 23, 27–37 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2828">25</a></sup>.</p><p>The gene dosage hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Birchler, J.A. &amp; Veitia, R.A. The gene balance hypothesis: from classical genetics to modern genomics. Plant Cell 19, 395–402 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2835">26</a></sup> predicts that gene functional categories encoding products that interact with one another or in networks should be over retained and genes with products that do not interact with other gene products should be under retained. In accordance with this hypothesis, we found <i>B. rapa</i> transcription factors with a detectable ortholog in <i>A. thaliana</i> to be significantly over retained (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). We obtained similarly consistent results for genes encoding known protein subunits of cytoplasmic ribosomes and for genes known to be involved with the proteosome. We found under retention of genes encoding products with few interactions, specifically those associated with DNA repair, nuclease activity, binding and the chloroplast (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 9</a>). The Gene Ontology annotation classes of over retained genes suggests that genome triplication may have expanded gene families that underlie environmental adaptability, as observed in other polyploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ha, M., Kim, E.D. &amp; Chen, Z.J. Duplicate genes increase expression diversity in closely related species and allopolyploids. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 2295–2300 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2855">27</a></sup>. Genes with Gene Ontology terms associated with response to important environmental factors, including salt, cold, osmotic stress, light, wounding, pathogen (broad spectrum) defense and both cadmium and zinc ions, were over retained (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>). Genes responding to plant hormones (jasmonic acid, auxin, salicylic acid, ethylene, brassinosteroid, cytokinin and abscisic acid) were also over retained.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The over retention genes in B. rapa showing strong bias."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: The over retention genes in <i>B. rapa</i> showing strong bias.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.919/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng919_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="499"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The <i>x</i> axis denotes the gene category. The <i>y</i> axis denotes the ratio of different copies in each category. The number of <i>B. rapa</i> orthologs of each class is indicated above each bar. RE, response to environment; RH, response to hormone; TF, transcription factor; CR, cytosolic ribosome; CW, cell wall. (<b>a</b>) The orange bar is the ratio of one- or two-copy orthologs, and the light green bar is the ratio of three copies. (<b>b</b>) The yellow bar is the ratio of one-copy orthologs, and the dark green bar is the ratio of two- or three-copy orthologs. The last category is the total sets of all orthologs listed as a control. The <i>P</i> value of each category is indicated under the bars. GO, Gene Ontology.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.919/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Under selection, <i>Brassica</i> species have a remarkable propensity for the development of morphological variants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Paterson, A.H., Lan, T.H., Amasino, R., Osborn, T.C. &amp; Quiros, C. Brassica genomics: a complement to, and early beneficiary of, the Arabidopsis sequence. Genome Biol. 2, R1011 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2911">28</a></sup>; we analyzed factors potentially involved in this development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). One factor may be a general acceleration of nucleotide substitution rates. For 2,275 orthologous groups of genes in <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, papaya and grape (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 10</a>), the nucleotide substitution rates in <i>B. rapa</i> were greater than in the other plants, with average <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> is the ratio of the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) and <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is the ratio of the number of non-synonymous substitutions per non-synonymous site) values 69% and 24%, respectively, greater than papaya and 1% and 7%, respectively, greater than <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 11</a>). The much slower evolutionary rate in papaya may be explained by its longer generation time as a perennial. Another factor may be expansion of auxin-related gene families, as auxin controls many plant growth and morphological developmental processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Teale, W.D., Paponov, I.A. &amp; Palme, K. Auxin in action: signalling, transport and the control of plant growth and development. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 7, 847–859 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2954">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Theologis, A. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 1 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 816–820 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2957">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Vanneste, S. &amp; Friml, J. Auxin: a trigger for change in plant development. Cell 136, 1005–1016 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2960">31</a></sup>. We identified 347 <i>B. rapa</i> genes related to auxin synthesis, transportation, signal transduction and inactivation, in contrast to 187 such genes present in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Figs. 5–14</a>). The TCP gene family is important in the evolution and specification of plant morphology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Reeves, P.A. &amp; Olmstead, R.G. Evolution of the TCP gene family in Asteridae: cladistic and network approaches to understanding regulatory gene family diversification and its impact on morphological evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 20, 1997–2009 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d8003e2980">32</a></sup>. This family has been amplified in <i>B. rapa</i>, which contains 39 TCP genes, which is more than <i>A. thaliana</i> (24), grape (19) or papaya (21) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). The regulation of flowering is key to many <i>Brassica</i> morphotypes. Mesohexaploidy has had contrasting effects on the genes involved. <i>FLC</i> (<i>FLOWERING LOCUS C</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Michaels, S.D. &amp; Amasino, R.M. FLOWERING LOCUS C encodes a novel MADS domain protein that acts as a repressor of flowering. Plant Cell 11, 949–956 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3004">33</a></sup> has three orthologs in <i>B. rapa</i> as a consequence of the WGT (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). Likewise, five of six <i>B. rapa VRN1</i> (<i>VERNALIZATION1</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Levy, Y.Y., Mesnage, S., Mylne, J.S., Gendall, A.R. &amp; Dean, C. Multiple roles of Arabidopsis VRN1 in vernalization and flowering time control. Science 297, 243–246 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3020">34</a></sup> produced by the WGT have been preserved (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). However, <i>GI</i> (<i>GIGANTEA</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Günl, M., Liew, E.F., David, K. &amp; Putterill, J. Analysis of a post-translational steroid induction system for GIGANTEA in Arabidopsis. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 141 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3034">35</a></sup> have been limited to only one copy (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>), as have the <i>SVP</i> (<i>SHORT VEGETATIVE PHASE</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li, D. et al. A repressor complex governs the integration of flowering signals in Arabidopsis. Dev. Cell 15, 110–120 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3048">36</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>) and each of the three <i>COL</i> (<i>CONSTANS-LIKE</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ledger, S., Strayer, C., Ashton, F., Kay, S.A. &amp; Putterill, J. Analysis of the function of two circadian-regulated CONSTANS-LIKE genes. Plant J. 26, 15–22 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3061">37</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>).</p><p>The comparison of the genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, as for previous comparisons of the cereals sorghum and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3079">38</a></sup>, sheds new light on the evolution of genome evolution in plants important for human nutrition. Our growing understanding of the processes shaping the triplicated genome of the mesopolyploid <i>B. rapa</i> is of relevance not only for closely related crops species, such as <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. napus</i>, but also for other important crops with triplicated genomes, such as bread wheat.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">URLs.</h3><p><i>Brassica</i> info, <a href="http://www.brassica.info/">http://www.brassica.info/</a>; GenoScope database, <a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/GenomeBrowser/Vitis/">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/GenomeBrowser/Vitis/</a>; Hawaii Papaya Genome Project, <a href="http://asgpb.mhpcc.hawaii.edu/papaya/">http://asgpb.mhpcc.hawaii.edu/papaya/</a>; <i>Arabidopsis</i> Information Resource, <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/">http://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>Approximately 72-fold shotgun coverage was generated using Illumina GA II sequencing from short (<span class="stix">∼</span>200 bp), medium (<span class="stix">∼</span>500 bp) and long (<span class="stix">∼</span>2 kb, 5 kb and 10 kb) insert libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 14</a>). The raw Illumina reads were filtered for duplicates, adaptor contamination and low quality before assembly into preliminary scaffolds using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3152">39</a></sup> run with default parameters. We first assembled the reads from the short insert size (≤500 bp) libraries into contigs using Kmer (de bruijn graph kmer) overlap information and ensured the resulting contigs were unique by determining an unambiguous path in the de bruijn graph. This resulted in contigs with an N50 length of 1.1 kb, achieving a total length of 222 Mb; the long insert size mate-paired libraries (≥2 kb) were not used initially because the chimaeric reads common to such libraries can generate incorrect sequence overlaps. After obtaining the unique contigs, we mapped all available paired-end reads to these contigs to connect adjacent contigs. In order to avoid interleaving and to reduce the impact of the insert-size deviation of any sequencing library, we used a hierarchical assembly method, constructing the scaffolds step by step by adding data from each library separately ranked according to insert size from smallest to largest. This obtained scaffolds with an N50 length of 347 kb and a total genome length of 288 Mb. Most of the remaining gaps between contigs probably occur in repetitive regions, so we identified the paired-end reads with only one end mapped to a unique contig and performed local assembly with the unmapped end to fill small gaps within the scaffolds. The resulting assembly had a final contig N50 length of 27 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 15</a>). In total, 32-Mb gaps were closed. A total of 199,452 BAC-end Sanger sequences retrieved from <a href="http://www.brassica-rapa.org/BRGP/bacEndList.jsp">http://www.brassica-rapa.org/BRGP/bacEndList.jsp</a> were used to construct the super scaffolds. The gaps within the scaffolds were filled in as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3167">40</a></sup>. The expected genome size of <i>B. rapa</i> was estimated from the distribution of 17-mer depth as assessed from the filtered sequence data using methods previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3174">40</a></sup>. The peak depth of 17-mers was at 15-folds and a total 7,287,899,150 17-mers were obtained. We obtained an estimated genome size of 485 Mb by dividing the total number of 17-mers by the peak depth.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Validation of assembly.</h3><p>NUCmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Delcher, A.L., Phillippy, A., Carlton, J. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Fast algorithms for large-scale genome alignment and comparison. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 2478–2483 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3186">41</a></sup> was used to compare the sequence of chromosome A03 assembled here by whole-genome shotgun sequencing (WGS A03) to the same chromosome assembled by BAC Sanger sequencing (BAC A03) previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mun, J.H. et al. Sequence and structure of Brassica rapa chromosome A3. Genome Biol. 11, R94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3190">15</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>). The total sizes of WGS A03 and BAC A03 are approximately 31.72 Mb and 32.70 Mb, respectively, with slightly more repeat sequences assembled using the BAC approach (9.82 Mb in BAC A03 and 5.68 Mb in WGS A03) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 16</a>). There were more gaps observed in BAC A03 (1,035/1,358,889 bp, number of gaps/total size of gaps) than in WGS A03 (858/844,319 bp) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 17</a>). We identified 44 obvious inversions (&gt;1 kb) between the two assemblies. Evidence provided by studying the mapped paired ends, the depth of the mapped reads and gaps at the boundaries for 38 inversions supported the WGS assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 22a,b</a>), and 6 inversions remained ambiguous (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 23c</a>). To evaluate the accuracy of the assembly on a local scale, the sequence of 647 complete BAC clones (phase 2 and phase 3) that had been deposited in NCBI and had been genetically mapped (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Sec3">URLs</a>) were compared with their equivalent WGS sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 18</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Integration of shotgun assembly with genetic maps.</h3><p>The scaffolds were anchored to the <i>B. rapa</i> genetic linkage map using 1,427 uniquely aligned markers from an integrated linkage map developed from four populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 19</a>). In addition, 1,054 markers mapped to the <i>B. napus</i> A genome were used to verify and aid the alignment. Chromosomes were orientated by alignment to the reference A genome linkage groups from Parkin <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Parkin, I.A. et al. Segmental structure of the Brassica napus genome based on comparative analysis with Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetics 171, 765–781 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3239">42</a></sup> (equivalent to N1-N10). Where genetic information was not available from <i>Brassica</i> maps, scaffold order and/or orientation was inferred based on evidence of conserved collinearity with the <i>A. thaliana</i> gene order.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Protein coding gene annotation.</h3><p>In addition to available <i>Brassica</i> EST data (downloaded from dbEST at NCBI 10 July 2010), we generated a total of 27.1 million Illumina RNA-Seq paired-end reads, 19.9 million of which were from Chiifu-401-42 and 7.2 million of which were from a Caixin accession, L58, to verify the predicted gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>). For Chiifu-401-42, equally mixed total RNA isolated from eight different tissues and growth conditions was used: leaves, roots and floral stems from plants grown in pots; 2-week-old etiolated seedlings; shoots from plants grown hydroponically under normal conditions; and leaves from plants treated with 0.5% NaCl at 4 °C and 37 °C for 24 h. For L58, equally mixed total RNA was isolated from similar tissues with the addition of germinating seeds, callus and pods.</p><p>The genome assembly was premasked for class I and class II transposable elements, and Genscan and Augustus were used to carry out <i>de novo</i> predictions with gene model parameters trained from <i>A. thaliana</i>. Genes with less than 150 bp of coding sequence were filtered out. For homology-based gene prediction, we aligned <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i> protein sequences to the <i>B. rapa</i> genome using TBLASTN (at an <i>E</i> value of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) for fast alignment and Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3297">43</a></sup> for precise alignment. The Unigene sequences of <i>B. rapa</i> and the <i>Brassica</i> ESTs downloaded from NCBI were aligned to the <i>B. rapa</i> genome using BLAT and assembled by PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3311">44</a></sup> based on genomic location. As the fragmental exons in ESTs data might lead to pseudo alignments, we filtered out the results with intron(s) more than 10,000 bp. GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3315">45</a></sup> was used to combine <i>de novo</i> gene sets and homology-based gene sets and incorporated the expressed sequence data described above as supporting evidence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Tables 20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#MOESM14">Supplementary Figs. 24 and 25</a>). In addition, those predicted <i>B. rapa</i> proteins that aligned to the Repbase transposable element protein database (<i>E</i> value 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup> at ≥50%) were filtered out.</p><p>The <i>B. rapa</i> predicted proteins were annotated based on alignment to the Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL databases with BLASTP at <i>E</i> value 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>. InterPro was used to annotate motifs and domains by comparison with publicly available databases including Pfam, PRINTS, PROSITE, ProDom and SMART. The Gene Ontology information for each gene code was extracted from the InterPro results.</p><p>To identify and estimate the number of potential orthologous gene families between <i>B. rapa, V. vinifera</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. papaya</i>, we applied the OrthoMCL pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3363">46</a></sup> using standard settings (BLASTP <i>E</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) to compute the all-against-all similarities.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Inter- and intra-genomic alignments.</h3><p>The synteny within and between species was constructed by McScan (MATCH_SCORE: 50, MATCH_SIZE: 5, GAP_SCORE: −3, E_VALUE: 1E–05). An all-against-all BLASTP comparison provided the pairwise gene information and <i>P</i> value for a primary clustering. Then, paired segments were extended by identifying clustered genes using dynamic programming. This method was used to build the genome synteny blocks of <i>B. rapa</i> versus <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i> versus <i>A. thaliana.</i></p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Phylogenetic analyses of biologically important gene families.</h3><p>Gene sequences from grape, papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i> were downloaded from the GenoScope database, the Hawaii Papaya Genome Project and the <i>Arabidopsis</i> Information Resource, respectively (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.919#Sec3">URLs</a>). Previously reported <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Brassica</i> gene sequences were downloaded from GenBank. The protein sequences of the genes were used to determine homologs in grape, papaya, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> by performing BLASTP searches at <i>E</i> value 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>. Alignment of each family was performed by running MEGA with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA4: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596–1599 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3447">47</a></sup> and subjected to careful manual checking to remove highly divergent sequences from further analysis. The retrieved protein sequences were used to reconstruct phylogenies using the neighbor-joining approach implemented in MEGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA4: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596–1599 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.919#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d8003e3451">47</a></sup>. A bootstrapping test was performed using 100 repetitive samplings for each gene family.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Accession codes.</h3><p>This whole-genome shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession <a href="http://getentry.ddbj.nig.ac.jp/getentry?database=ddbj&amp;accession_number=AENI00000000">AENI00000000</a>. The version described in this paper is the first version, <a href="http://getentry.ddbj.nig.ac.jp/getentry?database=ddbj&amp;accession_number=AENI01000000">AENI01000000</a>. Full annotation is available at <a href="http://brassicadb.org/">http://brassicadb.org/</a>.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature01521" aria-label="View reference 5">Article</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 5 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Unravelling%20angiosperm%20genome%20evolution%20by%20phylogenetic%20analysis%20of%20chromosomal%20duplication%20events&amp;journal=Nature&amp;volume=422&amp;pages=433-438&amp;publication_year=2003&amp;author=Bowers%2CJE&amp;author=Chapman%2CBA&amp;author=Rong%2CJ&amp;author=Paterson%2CAH">
                    Google Scholar</a>
